You have read that the consensus of Muslims affirms the sainthood of scholars like al-Sawi. Do you personally accept that such saints exist today, and does that acceptance change how you interact with the living scholars in your community? If not, what is holding you back from seeing them as potential bridges to Allah?

A Later Scholar's Tribute to al-Sawi
Praise of al-Sawi and description of his works

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly stature of al-Sawi as described by a later authority and the nature of his written works.

Core Idea
Al-Sawi was both a verifier and a spiritual guide; his works blend 'irfan with commentary.

This passage presents a second biographical appraisal of al-Sawi from Muhammad Makhluf, a later Maliki scholar. It reinforces al-Sawi's dual role as a juristic authority and a spiritual mentor, and highlights that his literary output consists mainly of glosses and commentaries infused with gnostic themes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow. Then read the translation to understand the meaning.
2
Consider the titles given to al-Sawi
Notice the long list of honorifics: imam, jurist, shaykh of shaykhs, etc. These indicate his high status in both law and spirituality.
Look for terms like 'قدوة السالكين' (role model of wayfarers) and 'مربي المريدين' (educator of disciples).
3
Understand the nature of his works
Makhluf says most of al-Sawi's works are glosses and commentaries. He emphasizes that they are beneficial, novel, and infused with 'irfan (divine gnosis).
The phrase 'في ربط وثيق لا ترى فيه إغرابا' means they are coherent without strangeness.

وقال العلامة محمد مخلوف المالكي التونسي في كتابه «شجرة النور الزكية في طبقات المالكية: (أبو العباس أحمد الصاوي الخلوتي، الإمام الفقيه شيخ الشيوخ، وعمدة أهل التحقيق والرسوخ، العلامة المحقق الحبر الفهامة المدقق، قدوة السالكين، ومربي المريدين) (۲). مؤلفاته: خلف الشيخ الإمام الصاوي كتباً متنوعة العلوم كما سترى، وأكثرها على طريقة التحشية والشرح وهي بحمد الله تعالى نافعة بديعة تتجلى في ثناياها كلمات العرفان وأهل الخصوصية في ربط وثيق لا ترى فيه إغراباً، وقد وردت أسماء مؤلفاته في مناقب الصاوي» مع بيان دواعيها وموضوعاتها (۳).
And the scholar Muhammad Makhluf al-Maliki al-Tunisi said in his book 'Shajarat al-Nur al-Zakiyya fi Tabaqat al-Malikiyya': 'Abu al-Abbas Ahmad al-Sawi al-Khalwati, the imam, the jurist, the shaykh of shaykhs, the pillar of the people of verification and firmness, the distinguished scholar, the verifier, the profound scholar, the exacting, the role model of the wayfarers, and the educator of the murids (disciples).' Most of them are in the form of glosses and commentaries, and they are, praise be to Allah, beneficial and novel; in their folds shine the words of divine gnosis and the people of special closeness, in a firm connection in which you see no strangeness. And the names of his works have been recorded in the book 'Manaqib al-Sawi,' along with their reasons and subjects. (3)

Commentary
This passage is a commendation from another notable scholar, Muhammad Makhluf, who describes al-Sawi with a string of honorific titles emphasizing his roles as an imam, jurist, the shaykh of shaykhs, a leading authority in verification (tahqiq) and firmness (rusukh), and a spiritual guide (qudwa al-salikin and murabbi al-muridin). The passage then turns to a description of al-Sawi's literary output. His works span various sciences and are predominantly glosses (hashiya) and commentaries (sharh). They are characterized by being beneficial and novel, embedding the discourse of gnosis and special divine closeness in a coherent manner without oddity. The specifics of his works are said to be listed in a separate biographical work 'Manaqib al-Sawi', with details on their motivations and subjects.

The Biography and Sainthood of Imam Ahmad al-Sawi
Biography praising Imam al-Sawi with consensus and testimony.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a saint's rank can be established by the consensus of Muslims rather than solely by reported miracles.

Core Idea
Consensus of Muslims suffices to establish a saint's rank.

Imam Ahmad al-Sawi was a towering figure in Islamic scholarship and spirituality. This brief biography from the commentary on the Kharida not only introduces his life but also teaches an important principle: sainthood (wilaya) is recognized through the unanimous testimony of the community, not just through extraordinary miracles. As you read the passage, notice how the author uses both a subtle miracle story and explicit scholarly consensus to affirm al-Sawi's status.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the two main sections: the story about the telegraph and the statement about consensus.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the miracle story
Notice the anecdote from Sheikh Husayn al-Jisr: news of al-Sawi's father's death reached Egypt from Syria without telegraph or mail. This hints at a miraculous event (kashf or karama).
The author does not emphasize the miracle; he uses it as a background detail.
3
Understand the consensus argument
The author states that al-Sawi does not need recounted miracles because the consensus (ijma) of Muslims places him among the greatest saints and scholars. This is a key theological point: consensus itself is a proof of sainthood.
Consensus is a strong evidence in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.
4
Reflect on the testimonial
Read the quote from Muhammad Makhluf's biographical dictionary, which lists al-Sawi's lofty titles. This is another form of testimony reinforcing the consensus.
These titles show the high regard al-Sawi held in the eyes of later scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمة الإمام الصاوي
من الزمان ورد لوالدي الخبر بوفاة والده رحمهم الله تعالى، هذا ولا يخفى أنه في ذلك الزمان لم يكن تلغراف ولا بريد منتظم بين مصر والشام، انتهى كلام الشيخ حسين الجسر حفظه الله .
ومثل الشيخ أحمد الصاوي المذكور لا يحتاج للدلالة على ولايته وكثرة فضله بنقل كثير من كراماته فإنه كان بإجماع المسلمين من أكابر أئمة العلماء العاملين الهادين المهديين ، وأئمة الأولياء العارفين المرشدين الكاملين، والله ينفعنا ببركاتهم (آمين)(۱)
وقال العلامة محمد مخلوف المالكي التونسي في كتابه «شجرة النور الزكية في طبقات المالكية: (أبو العباس أحمد الصاوي الخلوتي، الإمام الفقيه شيخ الشيوخ، وعمدة أهل التحقيق والرسوخ، العلامة المحقق الحبر الفهامة المدقق، قدوة السالكين، ومربي المريدين) (۲) .
Biography of Imam al-Sawi
From time, news of the death of his father, may Allah have mercy on them, reached my father. This, and it is not hidden that at that time there was no telegraph nor regular mail between Egypt and Syria. End of the speech of Sheikh Husayn al-Jisr, may Allah preserve him.
And such as the aforementioned Sheikh Ahmad al-Sawi has no need for indication of his sainthood and abundance of his virtue by transmitting many of his miracles, for he was by consensus of the Muslims among the greatest leaders of the acting scholars, the guides, the guided ones, and the leaders of the saints, the knowers, the guides, the perfect ones. And may Allah benefit us with their blessings. (Amin) (1)
And the distinguished scholar Muhammad Makhluf al-Maliki al-Tunisi said in his book 'Shajarat al-Nur al-Zakiyya fi Tabaqat al-Malikiyya': (Abu al-Abbas Ahmad al-Sawi al-Khalwati, the imam, the jurist, the sheikh of sheikhs, and the pillar of the people of verification and deep-rootedness, the distinguished scholar, the investigator, the erudite, the insightful, the role model of the seekers, and the nurturer of the disciples) (2).
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a biography of Imam Ahmad al-Sawi. It begins with a quote from Sheikh Husayn al-Jisr recounting that news of the death of al-Sawi's father reached his own father at a time when there was no telegraph or regular mail, hinting at a miraculous occurrence. The author then asserts that al-Sawi does not need to rely on narrations of miracles to prove his sainthood, because he is unanimously recognized by Muslims as one of the greatest acting scholars, guides, and saints. The passage then quotes the distinguished scholar Muhammad Makhluf al-Maliki al-Tunisi from his biographical work, listing al-Sawi's titles: imam, jurist, sheikh of sheikhs, pillar of verifiers, distinguished scholar, investigator, erudite, insightful, role model of seekers, and nurturer of disciples. This biography establishes al-Sawi's high spiritual and scholarly rank through consensus and authoritative testimony.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sainthood (wilaya) must be proven by miraculous deeds (karamat) alone.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that consensus (ijma') of the Muslim community is sufficient to establish a person's sainthood, as seen with Imam al-Sawi.

Key Takeaway

In your own life, do you seek signs from Allah or do you trust the recognition of the righteous? How can you train your heart to rely on the latter?
?
The anecdote of news reaching without telegraph hints at a miraculous event, but the author does not dwell on it. Could you overlook a miracle you witness and instead focus on the deeper reality? How attached are you to the extraordinary, and can you see the ordinary as a sign of Allah's favor?

Imam al-Sawi's Scholarly Legacy
Enumeration of al-Sawi's writings with brief descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the breadth and character of Imam al-Sawi's literary output, particularly his focus on glosses and commentaries on the works of his teacher al-Dardir.

Core Idea
Imam al-Sawi's works span jurisprudence, theology, and spirituality, mostly as commentaries.

Imam al-Sawi's bibliography reveals a scholar deeply engaged with his teacher al-Dardir's tradition. His works, mostly glosses and commentaries, are praised as beneficial and spiritually profound. This lesson surveys his key writings as listed in his biography, providing a window into his scholarly method.

Guided Path
1
Read the complete list
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview of the works. Notice the variety of topics: from litanies to jurisprudence to refutations.
The list includes both major commentaries and shorter treatises.
2
Identify the types of works
Categorize the works: some are glosses (حاشية) on al-Dardir's texts, others are independent explanations (شرح), and a few are original treatises (رسالة).
Most are connected to his teacher al-Dardir.
3
Reflect on the first authored work
The passage highlights that al-Sawi's first work was a refutation of those denying saints' miracles, written in one night. Consider what this tells us about his priorities.
This shows his concern for defending spiritual realities.

His Works
The Sheikh, Imam al-Sawi, left behind books of various sciences as you will see, and most of them are in the manner of gloss and commentary, and they are, praise be to Allah, beneficial, exquisite, manifesting in their folds the words of divine knowledge and the people of speciality in a strong connection in which you see no oddity. And the names of his works have been mentioned in 'Manaqib al-Sawi' with an explanation of their motivations and subjects (3):
- The Divine Secrets and the Merciful Emanations on the Dardiriyya Prayers.
- The Precious Unique Pearls on the Text of the Hamziyya, in which he explained the Hamziyya of Imam al-Busiri, may Allah have mercy on him.
-
—
Explanation of Tuhfat al-Ikhwan in the Science of Bayan and the original is by his teacher al-Dardir. Bulghat al-Salik li-Aqrab al-Masalik, and it is known as 'Hashiyat al-Sawi ala al-Sharh al-Saghir' and al-Sharh al-Saghir is the commentary of his teacher al-Dardir on his book 'Aqrab al-Masalik li-Madhhab al-Imam Malik'.
,
- Explanation of the Manzuma of the Beautiful Names of Allah, and the original is also by his teacher al-Dardir. - Gloss on the Commentary of al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya, and the poem 'al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya' and its commentary are both by his teacher al-Dardir, and this is our book.
- Explanation of the Poem Jawharat al-Tawhid and the original is by the distinguished scholar Ibrahim al-Laqani, may Allah have mercy on him. - Treatise on the Refutation of the Deniers of the Miracles of the Saints, he authored it in one complete night, and the reason for its authorship was a man's denial of miracles in the presence of Sheikh al-Dardir, and al-Dardir listened to it and said: 'It is as if he wrote it from my chest.' And this treatise is the first thing he authored, may Allah have mercy on him.
- Treatise on Jihad.
- Glorious Explanation on the Prayer (Yasin).
- Annotations on Dala'il al-Khayrat.
- Treatise on the Conditions and Etiquettes of Seclusion.
- Gloss on the Abridgment of al-Bukhari.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage enumerates the works of Imam al-Sawi. It begins with an introductory sentence noting that his writings cover various sciences, mostly in the form of glosses and commentaries, which are beneficial and exquisite, reflecting deep spiritual knowledge. The sources for the list are cited from the work 'Manaqib al-Sawi'. Then follows a list of his compositions: some on litanies and prayers, such as a commentary on the Dardiriyya prayers and a gloss on al-Busiri's Hamziyya; works on Arabic rhetoric (Tuhfat al-Ikhwan) and Maliki jurisprudence (Bulghat al-Salik); explanations of the Divine Names and a gloss on al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya (which is the book at hand); a commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid, an enduring creedal poem; and a treatise refuting deniers of saints' miracles, which he wrote in one night and which was his first authored work according to the text. The list also includes works on jihad, a commentary on the Prayer of Yasin, annotations on Dala'il al-Khayrat, a treatise on the conditions of spiritual seclusion, and a gloss on the abridgment of Sahih al-Bukhari. This enumeration demonstrates the breadth of al-Sawi's scholarship across jurisprudence, theology, spirituality, and hadith.

Imam al-Sawi's Scholarly Works: A Survey
Enumerates the scholarly works authored by Imam al-Sawi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the breadth and focus of Imam al-Sawi's literary output and his strong reliance on the works of his teacher, al-Dardir.

Core Idea
Al-Sawi's works mirror his teacher's influence and span theology, law, prayer, and refutations.

A scholar is often known by his teachers and his books. Imam al-Sawi, author of the present work, was a prolific writer whose compositions reflect his deep connection to his teacher al-Dardir. This list, found in a biographical note, introduces us to the range of subjects he addressed—from jurisprudence and theology to prayers and refutations. As you read through it, note how many of these works are commentaries or glosses on al-Dardir's own writings.

Guided Path
1
Read the full list of works
Read the Arabic list and its translation once to get a sense of the variety. Don't memorize; just observe the titles and subjects.
Notice that many titles include the name 'al-Dardir' or references to his books.
2
Identify works connected to al-Dardir
Go through the list and note which works are directly based on al-Dardir's originals: the prayers, the poem on names, the Sharh al-Saghir, al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya, the mawlid, etc.
This shows al-Sawi's role as a student who built upon his teacher's legacy.
3
Categorize the remaining works by subject
Group works that are not directly tied to al-Dardir: tafsir glosses (Baydawi, Jalalayn), hadith (Mukhtasar al-Bukhari), poetry commentary (Banat Su'ad, Hamziyya), and independent treatises (jihad, seclusion, refutation of miracle deniers).
This reveals al-Sawi's broader interests in Qur'an, hadith, Sufi literature, and contemporary debates.
4
Reflect on the significance of the list
Ask yourself why such a list appears in a biography. It establishes al-Sawi's scholarly authority and shows how a 19th-century Muslim scholar could range across disciplines.
It also reminds us that our present book is just one of many contributions by its author.

- حاشية على الجلالين.
The divine secrets and the merciful effusions on al-Dardir's prayers. - The precious pearls on the text of al-Hamziyya, commenting on al-Busiri's Hamziyya for Allah. - - — Explanation of Tuhfat al-Ikhwan in the science of eloquence and its original by his teacher al-Dardir. Bulghat al-Salik li-Aqrab al-Masalik, known as 'Hashiyat al-Sawi ala al-Sharh al-Saghir' and the Sharh al-Saghir is the commentary of his teacher al-Dardir on his book 'Aqrab al-Masalik li-Madhhab al-Imam Malik'. , - Commentary on the poem of the Beautiful Names of Allah, also originally by his teacher al-Dardir. - Gloss on the commentary of al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya, and the poem 'al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya' and its commentary both by his teacher al-Dardir, and this is our book. - Commentary on the poem Jawharat al-Tawhid, originally by the scholar Ibrahim al-Laqqani. - Treatise on refuting deniers of the miracles of saints, written in one complete night, and the reason for its authorship was a man's denial of miracles in the presence of Sheikh al-Dardir, and al-Dardir heard it and said: As if he wrote it from my chest, and this treatise is the first thing he authored. - Treatise on jihad. - Majestic commentary on the supplication (Ya Sin). - Notes on Dala'il al-Khayrat. - Treatise on the conditions and etiquette of seclusion. - Gloss on Mukhtasar al-Bukhari. - Gloss on the poem 'Banat Su'ad'. - Gloss on the Mawlid of his teacher al-Dardir. - Treatise on explaining the Basmala. - Gloss on Tafsir al-Qadi al-Baydawi. - Gloss on al-Jalalayn.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a biographical enumeration of the works of Imam al-Sawi. The list includes: 1) 'al-Asrar al-Rabbaniyya' - a commentary on al-Dardir's prayers. 2) 'al-Fara'id al-Saniyya' - commentary on al-Busiri's Hamziyya. 3) 'Sharh Tuhfat al-Ikhwan' - on eloquence, original by al-Dardir. 4) 'Bulghat al-Salik' - known as his gloss on al-Dardir's Sharh al-Saghir on Maliki jurisprudence. 5) Commentary on the poem of Allah's Names, also based on al-Dardir. 6) Gloss on al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya - the present book. 7) Commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid by al-Laqqani. 8) Treatise refuting deniers of saints' miracles, written in one night after an incident with al-Dardir. 9) Treatise on jihad. 10) Commentary on the supplication 'Ya Sin'. 11) Notes on Dala'il al-Khayrat. 12) Treatise on seclusion conditions. 13) Gloss on Mukhtasar al-Bukhari. 14) Gloss on 'Banat Su'ad'. 15) Gloss on al-Dardir's Mawlid. 16) Treatise on Basmala. 17) Gloss on Tafsir al-Baydawi. 18) Gloss on Tafsir al-Jalalayn. This list shows al-Sawi's diverse scholarly output and his strong connection to his teacher al-Dardir.

Key Terms
الخريدة البهية
جوهرة التوحيد
كرامات الأولياء
الدردير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The list might be mistaken for a table of contents of the present book (al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya) or a list of books by al-Dardir.
Correction: It is actually a list of works authored by Imam al-Sawi himself, arranged biographically to show his scholarly career. Only one of them (the gloss on al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya) is the book in our hands.

Checkpoint
What was the occasion for al-Sawi's treatise on the miracles of saints (كرامات الأولياء)?
A) A request from his teacher al-Dardir.
B) A man denied miracles in front of al-Dardir.
C) He dreamt about the Prophet.
D) He wanted to refute the philosophers.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Sawi authored a wide range of works, many of them commentaries on his teacher al-Dardir, reflecting his deep connection to the Maliki and Ash'ari tradition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imam al-Sawi defended the belief in karamat in a single night after hearing a denial. Is there a truth of the religion that you have remained silent about because it would be inconvenient? When will you speak or write in its defense?
?
His life's work was built on the foundation laid by his teacher al-Dardir. Whose guidance are you building upon? What are you producing that will honor those who taught you?
?
He authored over a dozen works spanning theology, law, and spirituality. If you were to leave behind a legacy of knowledge, what would it be? What is the first step you will take today to begin that legacy?

The Last Journey and Death of Imam al-Sawi
Describes the final journey and death of Imam al-Sawi in Medina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual significance of Imam al-Sawi's final journey to Hajj and his death in Medina, including his foreboding of his passing.

Core Idea
Imam al-Sawi's death was a foretold reunion with his beloved Prophet.

This lesson recounts the final days of Imam al-Sawi, a great scholar of the later Islamic tradition. His Hajj journey and subsequent illness in Medina reflect deep spiritual awareness, as he repeatedly hinted that his time was near. The account illustrates the profound love and connection between a saint and the Prophet Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Let the narrative unfold as a story.
Notice the emotional tone: the imam's words distressed his companions.
2
Identify key events
List the main events: setting out for Hajj, repeating 'the time is near', completing Hajj, visiting Medina, falling ill, and passing away on 7th Muharram.
The phrase 'حبيبه الأوحد' refers to the Prophet Muhammad.
3
Reflect on the spiritual meaning
Think about why the imam's words caused distress. Consider the concept of a saint being 'called' by the Prophet and the peace of dying in Medina.
This event is a testament to the imam's closeness to Allah and His Messenger.
4
Consider the prayer at the end
Read the closing prayer: 'May Allah restore to us from his states and blessings what gladdens hearts.' Reflect on how the author seeks blessing from the imam's story.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شد الإمام رحاله للحج في سنة (١٢٤٠هـ)، وكان معه كوكبة من تلاميذه ومحبيه، وكان في أيامه هذه كثيراً ما يردد: (الوقت قرب وحبيبي دعاني)، وكان هذا الكلام سبب كرب أصحابه.
ثم إنه أتم مناسك الحج، وعجّل بالزيارة للمقام الأفخم والقبر المعظم في المدينة المنورة، وما هي إلا أيام حتى تمرَّض هناك، وفي السابع من شهر الله المحرم في مستهلّ سنة (١٢٤١هـ) لبّى نداء حبيبه الأوحد () وكان هذا في رياض المدينة المنورة على ساكنها أفضل الصلوات الزاكيات وأطيب التسليمات. الله وأعاد علينا جميعاً من أحواله وبركاته ما تبتهج به القلوب، آمين
The Imam set out for Hajj in the year (1240 AH), and with him was a group of his students and devotees, and during these days he would often repeat: (The time is near and my beloved has called me), and this speech became a cause of distress for his companions. Then he completed the rites of Hajj, and hastened to visit the Most Sublime Station and the Venerated Grave in Medina, and it was only days until he fell ill there, and on the seventh of the month of Allah al-Muharram at the beginning of the year (1241 AH) he responded to the call of his unique beloved (), and this was in the gardens of Medina, upon its inhabitant be the best pure prayers and the most excellent greetings. May Allah restore to us all from his states and blessings what gladdens hearts, Amen.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is an account of the death of Imam al-Sawi. It narrates that he traveled for Hajj in 1240 AH with a group of students and devotees. During that time, he repeatedly said 'The time is near and my beloved has called me,' which distressed his companions. After completing Hajj, he hurried to visit the Prophet's Mosque in Medina. He fell ill there and on the seventh of Muharram 1241 AH he answered the call of his 'unique beloved' (the Prophet Muhammad). The location was the gardens of Medina. The passage ends with a prayer asking Allah to grant the readers from the states and blessings of the Imam. This account highlights his piety and the spiritual foreboding of his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the phrase 'حبيبه الأوحد' refers to Allah.
Correction: In context, it refers to the Prophet Muhammad, as the visit was to his grave.

Checkpoint

B) 1241 AH
C) 1250 AH
D) 1235 AH

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Sawi's death in Medina after Hajj, foretold by him, exemplifies the profound love and spiritual communion between a saint and the Prophet Muhammad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imam al-Sawi felt the nearness of death and said 'my beloved has called me.' Do you live with that same awareness that your time is short? What would change in your life if you truly believed you were about to meet the Prophet?
?
The Imam hastened to visit the Prophet's grave after Hajj. Is your love for the Prophet urgent or postponed? What is one concrete step you will take this week to increase your connection to him?

Lists the known works of Imam al-Sawi.

Imam al-Sawi's works reflect deep engagement with Qur'an, Hadith, and spirituality.

Imam al-Sawi was a prolific scholar whose writings span tafsir, poetry commentary, and devotional works. In this lesson, we review the list of his known compositions, each a contribution to Islamic learning.

The Final Journey: Imam al-Sawi's Death During Hajj
Describes Imam al-Sawi's death during Hajj in Medina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the circumstances of Imam al-Sawi's death during Hajj and the spiritual significance of his final journey.

Core Idea
Imam al-Sawi's death in Medina after Hajj exemplifies longing to meet the Beloved.

The biography of Imam al-Sawi concludes with a moving account of his final pilgrimage and passing. His repeated phrase, 'The time is near and my beloved has called me,' reveals his spiritual state and the peaceful culmination of his life.

Guided Path
1

Reflect on the repeated phrase

This phrase caused distress to his companions.
3
Understand the blessed death
He died in Medina after completing Hajj and visiting the Prophet's grave. This is considered a blessed death, as he passed away in the holy city.
The passage ends with a prayer for blessings from his state.

The Invocation: Basmalah, Hamdalah, and Shahada
Al-Sawi opens with basmalah, hamdalah, shahada, and salawat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the components of traditional Islamic scholarly openings and their spiritual significance.

Core Idea
Every great work begins with praise and testimony, anchoring scholarship in worship.

The opening lines of al-Sawi's hashiah follow a time-honored tradition of beginning with divine names, praise, and prophetic testimony. This lesson unpacks these elements, showing how they orient the reader toward the purpose of the work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text aloud, noticing the rhythm and sequence of phrases.
Focus on the order: basmalah, hamdalah, shahada, salawat.
2
Identify each phrase and its purpose
List each distinct invocation: basmalah (seeking aid), hamdalah (praise), shahada (testimony), salawat (blessings).
3
Reflect on the order: why praise before testimony?
Consider why praise of Allah precedes the declaration of faith, and why testimony comes before blessings on the Prophet.
This order establishes tawhid as the foundation.
4
Understand how this sets the tone
Recognize that these openings are not mere formality; they align the work with divine purpose and scholarly humility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم وبه نستعين. الحمد لله رب العالمين، والشهادة أن لا إله إلا الله وحده لا شريك له، شهادة تبقى إلى يوم الدين، وأشهد أن سيدنا محمداً عبده ورسوله الصادق الأمين، صلى الله عليه وسلم وعلى آله وأصحابه والتابعين. أما بعد: فيقول المفتقر إلى ربه أحمد بن محمد المالكي الصاوي [?] شرح خريدة [?] إلى الشيخ أحمد الدردير [?] وقد كرا [?] في حال حياته وتلقاه عنه [?] قامت بنا الدواعي إلى ترانه [?] وشرعت الآن في ذلك راجياً من الله بلوغ المطالب متوسلاً بالنبي [?] إلى الله تعالى ناقول وهو المبي ونعم الوكيل.
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful, and by Him we seek aid. Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds, and the testimony that there is no god but Allah alone, no partner, a testimony that remains until the Day of Judgment, and I bear witness that our master Muhammad is His servant and messenger, the truthful and trustworthy, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and upon his family, companions, and followers. To proceed: the one in need of his Lord, Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Maliki al-Sawi [?] the explanation of the Kharidah [?] to Shaykh Ahmad al-Dardir [?] and he [?] during his lifetime and received it from him [?] the motives prompted us to [?] and I have now begun that, hoping from Allah attainment of goals, interceding through the Prophet [?] to Allah, and we say: He is the best of agents.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the hashiah. The author begins with the basmalah and hamdalah, following the traditional style of Islamic scholarly writing. He then recites the shahada and sends blessings upon the Prophet, his family, companions, and followers. He then says 'Amma ba'd' (to proceed) and introduces himself as 'the one in need of his Lord, Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Maliki al-Sawi.' He states his purpose: to write a gloss on the Sharh al-Kharidah of Shaykh Ahmad al-Dardir. He mentions that he was urged to do so by his teacher and that he began this work hoping for Allah's help and interceding through the Prophet. The closing phrase 'nāqūl wa huwa al-mubī wa ni‘m al-wakīl' means 'We say: He is the best of agents,' indicating his reliance on Allah. The passage establishes the author's humility and his dependence on divine aid.

Key Terms
بسملة
حمدلة
شهادة
استعانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Hamdalah
B) Basmalah
C) Shahada
D) Salawat

Key Takeaway
The opening invocations establish the work's foundation in divine praise and prophetic reverence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly begin your daily tasks with the basmalah, seeking Allah's aid, or do you often rely on your own strength and plans? What concrete change can you make today to start each activity consciously with 'Bismillah'?
?
When you recite the shahada, do you feel the weight of its permanence until the Day of Judgment, or has it become a hollow phrase? How would your life be different if you lived every moment with the conviction that this testimony will be your companion in the grave and on the Day of Resurrection?
?
The author sends salawat upon the Prophet, his family, and companions. Do you have such love and reverence for them that you regularly invoke blessings? If not, what is holding you back, and how can you cultivate that love starting now?

The Author's Purpose: A Gloss on al-Kharidah
Al-Sawi states his name, his work, and his reliance on Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's stated intent, his humility, and his reliance on divine aid.

Core Idea
The author presents himself as a servant in need, seeking help for a work rooted in his teacher's legacy.

After the traditional praises, al-Sawi introduces himself and his project. He humbly describes himself as 'al-muftaqir ila rabbih' (the one in need of his Lord) and states that he is writing a gloss on the Sharh al-Kharidah of Shaykh Ahmad al-Dardir. This lesson explores his statement of purpose and the accompanying prayer.

Guided Path

Al-Sawi's Motivation: A Dream Command and Teacher's Legacy
Al-Sawi introduces his hashaya with a personal narrative of his teacher's commentary and a dream command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the personal and spiritual motivations that led al-Sawi to write his hashaya on al-Dardir's commentary.

Core Idea
Al-Sawi's hashaya was born from a dream command and a teacher's spiritual transmission.

Every great scholarly work has a story behind it. Al-Sawi begins his hashaya not with a proof, but with a personal account: his teacher's commentary, the way he learned it, and a repeated dream that moved him to write. This introduction reveals the spiritual roots of Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Notice the classical opening formula and then the personal narrative.
The basmala and hamdala are standard, but then al-Sawi shifts to his personal motivation.
2
Identify the components
Break down the passage into its parts: basmala, hamdala, shahada, salawat, then the personal story. Note the phrase 'أما بعد' which transitions to the main content.
3
Understand the dream command
Focus on the sentence 'أمرني بذلك الأستاذ مناماً المرة بعد المرة'. Understand that al-Sawi claims his teacher commanded him in a dream repeatedly. This is a spiritual impetus, not a source of legislation.
The common misunderstanding is that this is a claim to prophecy; it is not.
4
Trace the chain of transmission
Notice the lineage: al-Sawi received from al-Dardir via 'al-ḥāl wal-qāl' (spiritual state and oral transmission). Then he seeks intercession through his teacher to the Prophet and through the Prophet to Allah. This shows the sanctity of the teacher-student relationship.
This tawassul is a common practice in traditional Islamic scholarship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة المحشي
بِسْمِ اللهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيمِ
وبه نستعين
الحمد لله ربِّ العالمين، وأشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وحده لا شريك له، شهادةً تبقى إلى يوم الدين، وأشهد أنَّ سيدنا محمداً عبده ورسوله الصادق الأمين، صلَّى الله وسلَّم عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه والتابعين .
أما بعد :
فيقول العبد الفقير الرَّاجي من ربه غفر المساوي، أحمد بن محمد المالكي الصَّاوي : لما كان شرح شيخنا وقدوتنا إلى الله تعالى أبي البركات الشَّيخ أحمد الدردير على رسالته المسماة بـ «الخريدة البهية» في علم التوحيد من أجل الشروح، وقد قرأه في حال حياته، وتلقيناه عنه بالحال والقال؛ قامت بنا الدواعي الإلهيَّة الآن إلى قراءته وخدمته، كما أمرني بذلك الأستاذ مناماً المرة بعد المرة، فشرعت الآن في ذلك؛ راجياً من الله بلوغ المطالب وحصول المآرب؛ متوسلاً بأستاذي إلى النَّبيِّ صلَّى الله عليه وسلّم، وبالنَّبي إلى الله تعالى.
فأقول وهو حسبي ونعم الوكيل :
Introduction of the Commentator: In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. And with Him we seek help. Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds, and I bear witness that there is no god but Allah alone, without partner, a testimony that remains until the Day of Judgment, and I bear witness that our master Muhammad is His servant and Messenger, the truthful and trustworthy. May Allah send prayers and peace upon him, his family, his companions, and the followers. To proceed: Thus says the poor servant, hoping from his Lord forgiveness of sins, Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Maliki al-Sawi: Since the commentary of our teacher and our guide to Allah Almighty, Abu al-Barakat al-Shaykh Ahmad al-Dardir, on his treatise named 'al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya' in the science of tawhid, is among the greatest commentaries, and he taught it during his lifetime, and we received it from him through spiritual state and spoken transmission; the divine motivations have now moved us to recite it and serve it, just as the master (teacher) commanded me in a dream repeatedly. So I have now begun that, hoping from Allah the attainment of goals and the realization of desires, seeking intercession through my teacher to the Prophet (peace be upon him), and through the Prophet to Allah Almighty. I say, and He is sufficient for me and the best Disposer.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is the personal introduction (muqaddima) of the commentator Ahmad al-Sawi to his hashaya on al-Dardir's commentary. It follows the classical format: basmala, hamdala, shahada, and salawat. Then al-Sawi identifies himself as 'al-ʻabd al-faqīr al-rājī' (the poor servant hoping for forgiveness). He states his motivation: the commentary of his teacher al-Dardir on 'al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya' in the science of tawhid is among the greatest commentaries. Al-Dardir taught it in his lifetime, and al-Sawi received it from him through 'al-ḥāl wal-qāl' (spiritual state and oral transmission). Divine motivations now impel him to read and serve it, especially since the master (al-Dardir) commanded him in a dream repeatedly. So he began this work, hoping for success, and seeking intercession (tawassul) through his teacher to the Prophet, and through the Prophet to Allah. He then says, 'fa-aqūlu wa huwa ḥasbī wa niʻma al-wakīl' (I say, and He is sufficient for me and the best Disposer). This introduction establishes the chain of transmission and the spiritual impetus for writing.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that al-Sawi's dream command is a form of revelation or legislation.
Correction: Al-Sawi does not claim the dream as a source of law; it is a personal spiritual encouragement. The dream is a motivation, not a proof for others.

Al-Sawi's Preface: Motivation and Methodology
Al-Sawi opens his hashya with praise, self-identification, and explanation of motivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Sawi's motivation for writing his hashya and the traditional scholarly etiquette displayed in the preface.

Core Idea
Al-Sawi writes not for fame but in obedience to a divine command revealed through a dream.

Before diving into theological commentary, al-Sawi sets the stage with a preface that reveals his scholarly lineage and spiritual motivation. This brief but rich opening shows us the etiquette of Islamic scholarship: beginning with praise, identifying oneself humbly, and explaining the inspiration behind the work. Al-Sawi's mention of a dream command from his teacher adds a personal dimension that connects the earthly chain of transmission to spiritual guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify components
Read the Arabic passage and translation, then identify the basmala, hamdala, shahada, and blessings on the Prophet and his followers.
These are standard openings in Islamic texts, but they set the tone for the work.
2
Notice the author's self-description
Al-Sawi calls himself 'the poor servant, hoping for forgiveness.' This reflects the humility expected of a scholar.
3
Understand the motivation
He praises his teacher al-Dardiri's sharh and mentions that he read it with him during his lifetime. Then he reveals the dream command that spurred him to write.
The phrase 'by state and speech' indicates both spiritual and verbal transmission.
4
Trace the chain of intercession
Al-Sawi uses his teacher as a means to the Prophet, and the Prophet as a means to Allah. This shows the traditional understanding of spiritual lineage.
5
Reflect on dream inspiration
Consider the role of dreams in Islamic scholarship: they are taken seriously as a form of divine guidance, especially when repeated.
This guards against the misconception that al-Sawi wrote for personal gain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بِسْمِ اللهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيمِ وبه نستعين الحمد لله ربِّ العالمين، وأشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وحده لا شريك له، شهادةً تبقى إلى يوم الدين، وأشهد أنَّ سيّدنا محمداً عبده ورسوله الصادق الأمين، صلَّى الله وسلَّم عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه والتابعين . أما بعد : فيقول العبد الفقير الرَّاجي من ربه غَفْر المساوي، أحمد بن محمد المالكي الصَّاوي : لمَّا كان شرح شيخنا وقدوتنا إلى الله تعالى أبي البركات الشَّيخ أحمد الدردير على رسالته المسماة بـ «الخريدة البهية» في علم التوحيد من أجل الشروح، وقد قرأه في حال حياته، وتلقيناه عنه بالحال والقال؛ قامت بنا الدواعي الإلهيَّة الآن إلى قراءته وخدمته، كما أمرني بذلك الأستاذ مناماً المرَّة بعد المرَّة، فشرعت الآن في ذلك؛ راجياً من الله بلوغ المطالب وحصول المآرب ؛ متوسلاً بأستاذي إلى النَّبيِّ صلَّى الله عليه وسلّم، وبالنَّبي إلى الله تعالى. فأقول وهو حسبي ونعم الوكيل :
In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful, and by Him we seek help. Praise be to God, Lord of the worlds, and I bear witness that there is no god but God alone, without partner, a testimony lasting until the Day of Judgment, and I bear witness that our master Muhammad is His servant and Messenger, the truthful and trustworthy. May God bless him and grant him peace, and upon his family, companions, and those who follow them. To proceed: the poor servant, hoping from his Lord forgiveness of sins, Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Maliki al-Sawi, says: Since the commentary of our shaykh and our guide to God Almighty, Abi al-Barakat al-Shaykh Ahmad al-Dardir, on his treatise called 'al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya' in the science of tawhid, is among the best commentaries, and I read it during his lifetime and received it from him by state and by speech; divine motivations have now arisen for me to read it and serve it, just as the teacher commanded me in a dream repeatedly. So I have now commenced that, hoping from God the attainment of objectives and the fulfillment of goals, making my teacher a means to the Prophet (peace be upon him), and through the Prophet to God Almighty. And I say—and He is sufficient for me and the best trustee:
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is the author's preface. It begins with the basmala and hamdala, followed by the shahada and blessings on the Prophet and his followers. After 'amma ba'd', al-Sawi identifies himself as a humble servant hoping for forgiveness. He explains his motivation: the sharh of his teacher al-Dardiri on the 'Kharidat al-Bahiyya' is excellent, and he studied it with the author while he was alive, receiving it 'by state and speech' (i.e., through both spiritual transmission and verbal instruction). Now divine impulses have moved him to read and serve this commentary, especially since his teacher commanded him in a dream repeatedly. Therefore, he has begun writing this hashya, seeking from Allah success and using his teacher as a means to the Prophet and the Prophet as a means to Allah. He concludes with the phrase 'And He is sufficient for me and the best trustee.'

The Honorific 'Sayyid' and Its Application to the Prophet
Al-Sawi explains the meaning and application of 'sayyid' to the Prophet, with linguistic and hadith support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Prophet is called 'Sayyid' and how this title is both honorific and limited, with true sayyidship belonging to Allah alone.

Core Idea
Only Allah is the absolute Sayyid; the Prophet's title is a borrowed honorific.

In this passage, al-Sawi unpacks the honorific 'sayyid' as applied to the Prophet Muhammad. He combines linguistic analysis, poetic evidence, and prophetic hadith to show that while the Prophet is truly a master over humanity, the ultimate mastery belongs to Allah. This lesson will guide you through the layers of meaning and the proper understanding of this important term.

Guided Path
1
Understand the grammatical structure
Notice how 'sayyid' is made definite by the external covenant and how 'Muhammad' is placed before it as a sign of independence.
The commentator highlights the departure from the normal attribute order.
2
Learn the linguistic definition and derivation
Read the linguistic definition of 'sayyid' as one excelling in generosity and forbearance, and trace the morphological derivation from 'sāda yasūdu'.
The poetic line illustrates the meaning.
3
Explore other usages of 'sayyid'
List the other meanings: one with a large army, leader of a community, and the perfect one needed in hardships.
All these meanings apply to the Prophet.
4
Examine the hadith evidence
Review the hadith 'I am the master of the children of Adam' and the correction that 'The sayyid is Allah'.
This establishes the theological boundary.
5
Grasp the exclusive sayyidship of Allah
Understand that only Allah is the absolute Sayyid, and the application to others is metaphorical (bi-tarīq al-‘āriyya).
Reflect on the wisdom behind this distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (على سيّدنا ) أي : أشرف بني آدم فهو سيد غيرهم بالأولى، والإضافة فيه لتعريف العهد الخارجي؛ أي: السَّيد المعيَّن المعلوم، وقدَّمه على (محمد) مع أنَّه صفةٌ له، والأصل تأخير الصفة عن الموصوف؛ إشارةً إلى استقلالها بنفسها حتى صارت كالعَلَم . والسَّيّد لغة: من فاق غيره كرماً وحلماً ، قال الشاعر (۱) : [من الطويل] ببذل وحلم ساد في قومه الفتى . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . (۱) صدر بيت وتمامه : وكونك إياه عليك يسير أورده ابن مالك في شرح تسهيل الفوائد (۳۳۹/۱) من غير نسبة . محمَّدِ من ساد يسود سيادةً فهو سيد، وأصله سيود بكسر الواو، قلبت ياءً؛ : لتحركها واجتماعها مع الياء الساكنة ،قبلها، ثمَّ أدغمت فيها؛ لاجتماع المثلين. والقاعدة: أنَّ الذي يدغم هو الذي يقلب ويردُّ من جنس المدغم فيه، لكن لمَّا كانت الياء أخفَّ من الواو قلبت الواو ياء مطلقاً . اللغة أيضاً على من كثر سواده؛ أي: جيشه، أو المتولي ويطلق في للسَّواد ؛ أي: الجماعة الكثيرة، وعلى الكامل المحتاج إليه عند وكلُّ هذه المعاني مناسبة لمقامه الشدائد، . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . وإطلاق السيّد عليه هل ورد في الأخبار ؛ منها : رواية أحمد والترمذي وابن ماجه عن أبي سعيد : «أنا سيد ولد آدمَ يوم القيامة ولا فخر»(۱)، وغير ذلك من الأحاديث المتواترة. وقوله لمن قال له يا سيّد: «السَّيِّدُ) هوَ الله (۲)، فمعناه أَنَّه : صلى الله عليه وسلم الحقيق بالسيادة، وإطلاقها على غيره إنَّما هو بطريق العارية؛ فالمقصود منه : إعلام الجاهل بالحقيقة، فتدبّر . كما :قوله : (محمَّد) بدل من سيّد، أو عطف بيان عليه جيء به للمدح كما يجيء النَّعت لذلك . (۱) سنن الترمذي (٣١٤٨) ، سنن ابن ماجه (٤۳۰۸)، مسند الإمام أحمد (۲/۳). (٢) أخرجه أبو داود (٤٨٠٦) ، والنسائي في السنن الكبرى» (۱۰۰۳) من حديث عبد الله بن الشخير الله
His saying: (upon our master) i.e., the noblest of the children of Adam, so he is master of others a fortiori. The addition here is for specifying the external covenant; i.e., the specific known master. And he preceded (Muhammad) even though it is an attribute of it, and the original rule is that the attribute follows the described; indicating its independence until it became like a proper name. And 'as-sayyid' linguistically: one who surpasses others in generosity and forbearance. The poet said: 'By generosity and forbearance a youth became leader among his people...' (The line and its completion: 'And your being him is easy upon you' quoted by Ibn Malik in Sharh Tashil al-Fawa'id 1/339 without attribution.) Muhammad: from 'sāda yasūdu sayyidatan' so he is sayyid. Its origin is 'saywad' with kasra on the waw, then the waw was turned into a ya because it moved and met a preceding silent ya, then the two were assimilated due to the meeting of the two similar letters. The rule: that which is assimilated is the one which is changed and returned to the genus of the assimilated, but since the ya is lighter than the waw, the waw is turned into ya absolutely. It is also used in the language for one whose population (sawād) is numerous, i.e., his army, or the one entrusted with leadership over the community, i.e., the large group, and for the perfect one who is needed in times of hardship. All these meanings are appropriate to his station. And the application of 'sayyid' to him has come in reports: among them the narration of Ahmad, al-Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah from Abu Sa'id: 'I am the master of the children of Adam on the Day of Resurrection and I am not boasting' and other mutawātir hadiths. And his saying to one who said to him 'O sayyid': 'The sayyid is Allah', so its meaning is that he, peace be upon him, is truly deserving of lordship, and its application to others is only by way of borrowing; the intended meaning is to inform the ignorant of the truth, so reflect. Also: his saying: (Muhammad) is a substitute for sayyid, or an explicative apposition, brought for praise just as the adjective is brought for that. (1) Sunan al-Tirmidhi (3148), Sunan Ibn Majah (4308), Musnad Ahmad (2/3). (2) Narrated by Abu Dawud (4806) and al-Nasa'i in al-Sunan al-Kubra (1003) from the hadith of Abdullah ibn al-Shikhkhir.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and hadith-based commentary on the honorific 'sayyid' as applied to Prophet Muhammad. The commentator begins by explaining the grammar: 'upon our master' indicates the noblest human, and the definite article specifies the known master. The word 'Muhammad' is placed before its attribute to show independence. He then gives the linguistic definition of 'sayyid' as one excelling in generosity and forbearance, citing a poetic line. The morphological derivation is traced: from 'sāda yasūdu', with the origin 'saywad' (with a waw) then converted to ya and assimilated, following a phonetic rule. The commentator lists other usages of 'sayyid': one with a large army (sawād), the ruler of a community, and the perfect person sought in hardships, all fitting the Prophet. He then cites hadiths: the famous 'I am the master of the children of Adam' and the Prophet's correction that 'The sayyid is Allah', establishing that the Prophet is truly deserving of the title, but its application to others is only metaphorical (bi-tarīq al-‘āriyya). The passage concludes by parsing 'Muhammad' grammatically as a substitute (badal) or explicative apposition (ʿaṭf bayān) for praise.

Key Terms
سيد
سيادة
عارية
محمد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That calling someone 'sayyid' is always absolute and unrestricted.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that only Allah is the absolute Sayyid, and the Prophet's title is a borrowed honorific (bi-tarīq al-‘āriyya).

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited, who is the absolute Sayyid?
A) The Prophet Muhammad
B) Allah
C) All believers
D) The righteous caliphs

Key Takeaway
The title 'sayyid' for the Prophet is a legitimate honorific, but absolute sayyidship belongs to Allah alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you use the word 'sayyid' for someone—scholar, leader, or even the Prophet—do you ever pause to reflect that the true Sayyid is Allah alone? How does that awareness change the way you honour creation?
?
The Prophet corrected the man who called him 'sayyid' by saying, 'The sayyid is Allah.' If you were that man, would your ego have allowed you to accept that correction without feeling that the Prophet was rejecting your praise? Can you think of a time you struggled to accept a similar correction about misplaced reverence?
?
You recite 'sayyidina Muhammad' multiple times daily. After this lesson, will you recite it with the conscious understanding that it is a borrowed honorific? Commit to one specific prayer or moment today where you will say it with that awareness.

Understanding 'Al' and 'Ashab': The Virtues of the Prophet's Family and Companions
The author defines 'Al' and 'Ashab' and extols the Companions' virtues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic meaning of 'Al' and 'Ashab' as used in Islamic texts and recognize the high status of the Companions as affirmed by the Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
The Companions are praised by Allah and His Messenger; their virtues are a source of pride.

This passage from the matn defines two key terms that appear frequently in Islamic texts: 'Al' (the Prophet's family) and 'Ashab' (his Companions). The author not only explains their linguistic form but also cites a hadith and references to the Quran to underscore the exceptional merits of the Companions. As you read, pay attention to the linguistic nuances and the theological implications of these titles.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith on blessings
Begin by reading the hadith from Anas ibn Malik. Notice how it emphasizes the forgiveness granted to one who sends blessings on the Prophet and his family.
This hadith is cited to highlight the virtue of invoking blessings on the Prophet's family.
2
Understand the definition of 'Al'
The author defines 'Al' as a collective noun with no singular from its own word. Its singular is understood from its meaning (e.g., 'ahl' meaning family).
This is a key linguistic point.
3
Analyze the plural 'Ashab'
Examine the grammatical explanation: 'Ashab' is an irregular plural of 'sahb', not 'sahib'. It follows the pattern for triliteral nouns with a weak medial letter.
Compare with examples like 'sayf/asyaaf'.
4
Learn the glosses on 'uli', 'manaqib', and 'fakhira'
These words are explained: 'uli' means possessors, 'manaqib' means perfections, and 'fakhira' means magnificent qualities that are boasted of in both worlds.
5
Reflect on the Quranic and prophetic praise
The author concludes that Allah and the Prophet have praised the Companions abundantly. Consider the theological significance of this widespread praise.
This reinforces the high status of the Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأصحابه أولي المناقب الفاخرة. وعن أنس بن مالك قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «من قالَ: اللَّهمَّ؛ صلِّ على محمَّدٍ وعلى آلِهِ، وكانَ قائماً؛ غُفِرَ له قبل أن يقعد، وإن كان قاعداً؛ غُفِرَ له قبل أن يقوم». والآل: اسم جمع باتفاق لا واحد له من لفظه، بل من معناه. قوله: (وأصحابه): جمع صحب على غير قياس؛ لأنَّ شرط اطراد جمع فَعْل - بفتح فسكون - على أفعال كونُ عينه حرف علة؛ كسيف وأسياف، وثوب وأثواب، وليس جمع صاحب؛ لأنَّ فاعلاً لا يجمع على أفعال، وإنَّما هو جمع اسم ثلاثي؛ كباب وأبواب. قوله: (أولي) أي: أصحاب. قوله: (المناقب): منقبة ضد المثلبة؛ أي: الكمالات. وقوله: (الفاخرة) أي: العظيمة التي يُفتخر بها دنيا وأخرى، وقد ذكر الله مناقبهم في غير آية ومدحهم الرَّسول في غير حديث.
And his companions, possessors of excellent merits. And from Anas ibn Malik, he said: The Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, said: 'Whoever says: O God, bless Muhammad and his family, and he was standing, he will be forgiven before he sits; and if he was sitting, he will be forgiven before he stands.' And 'Al' is a collective noun by agreement, having no singular from its own word, but rather from its meaning. His statement: 'and his companions' is a plural of 'sahb' irregularly, because the condition for the regular plural of fa'l (with fatha and sukun) onto af'al is that its medial letter be a weak letter, like sayf and asyaf, and thawb and athwab, and it is not the plural of sahib, because fa'il does not form a plural on af'al, rather it is the plural of a triliteral noun, like bab and abwab. His statement: 'uli' meaning: possessors. His statement: 'al-manaqib' is sing. manqaba, opposite of mathlaba, meaning: perfections. And his statement: 'al-fakhira' meaning: the magnificent ones, which are boasted of in this world and the next, and God has mentioned their merits in more than one verse, and the Messenger praised them in more than one hadith.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of key terms in the phrase 'and his companions, possessors of excellent merits' (وأصحابه أولي المناقب الفاخرة). The author first cites a hadith from Anas ibn Malik that highlights the virtue of invoking blessings on the Prophet and his family. He then defines 'Al' (آل) as a collective noun that has no singular from its own root; its singular is taken from its meaning (i.e., 'ahl' or family). Next, he explains that 'Ashab' (أصحاب) is an irregular plural of 'sahb', not 'sahib'; it follows the pattern of fa'l (فَعْل) even though 'sahib' is fa'il (فاعل). He clarifies that the plural on af'al (أفعال) is only regular for triliteral nouns with a weak medial letter, like sayf and thawb. Then he glosses 'uli' as 'possessors' and 'al-manaqib' as perfections (the opposite of defects), and 'al-fakhira' as magnificent qualities that are a source of pride in both worlds. He concludes by asserting that God and the Prophet have abundantly praised the Companions in multiple verses and hadiths. The commentary flows from the hadith to the lexical clarifications, each supporting the high status of the Prophet's family and Companions.

مناقب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'آل' has a singular form like 'آئل'.
Correction: The author states that 'آل' is a collective noun with no singular from its own word; its singular is understood from its meaning (such as 'أهل').

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's family (Al) and Companions (Ashab) are highly praised, and the terms have specific linguistic features that reflect their special status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'O God, bless Muhammad and his family', do you truly appreciate that the 'Al' are a unique collective without equal? How does this realization affect the weight of your prayer?
?
The Companions are described as possessing 'magnificent merits praised by Allah and His Messenger'. Do you carry that reverence in your heart when you hear their names, or have you allowed modern voices to diminish their status? What one action can you take today to honor them more deeply?
?
If the very word 'Ashab' is grammatically unique—not the plural of 'sahib' but a special irregular form—what does that suggest about their distinct rank? How can you ensure your treatment of the Sahabah reflects that they are not ordinary people?

Biography of Imam al-Dardir and His Father
Biographical description of Imam al-Dardir and a quote about his father.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the biographical background of Imam al-Dardir and the piety of his father as described in al-Sawi's commentary.

Core Idea
A scholar's lineage often shines through a parent's devotion.

In this passage, the commentator al-Sawi introduces us to the author of the original text—Imam al-Dardir—and then shares a touching portrait of his father. The father, despite losing his sight, dedicated his life to teaching the Quran for free, embodying selflessness and piety. This glimpse into the family background helps us appreciate the spiritual environment that shaped the imam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Note that 'Ahmad ibn Muhammad' is the name of the author (Imam al-Dardir). The passage explains that 'Ahmad' is his name and 'Muhammad' is his father's name.
This clarifies the full name of the imam.
2
Read the father's description
The father is described as a righteous scholar, Quran expert, who lost his sight late in life and then taught children the Quran without charging.
Notice the father's dedication despite blindness.
3
Reflect on the father's piety
He taught the poor for free, even helping them from his own means, was very silent, and his litany was the prayer of Abd al-Salam ibn Mashish.
This shows his spiritual discipline.
4
Note the glad tiding
The father gave the young al-Dardir the good news that he would become a scholar.
This connects the father's piety to the son's future.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أحمد) : هو اسم الشيخ. وقوله : (ابن محمّد) هو اسم أبيه قال الشَّيخ في شرح كتابه «أقرب المسالك لمذهب الإمام مالك»: وكان الوالد الله تعالى رجلاً صالحاً عالماً متقناً للقرآن، فقد بصره في آخر عمره، فاشتغل بتعليم الأطفال كتاب الله تعالى فحفظ القرآن على يديه خلق كثير . وكان يعلّم الفقراء حسبةً لله تعالى لا يأخذ منهم صرفةً ولا غيرها، بل ربَّما واساهم من عنده وكان كثير الشكوت لا يتكلم إلا نادراً، وورده في غالب أوقاته صلاة سيدي عبد السَّلام بن مشيش ، وكان يبشرني [في صغري بأن أكون عالماً .
His statement: (Ahmad): it is the name of the Shaykh. And his statement: (ibn Muhammad) is the name of his father. The Shaykh said in his commentary on his book 'Aqrab al-Masalik li-Madhhab al-Imam Malik': 'And the parent (may God have mercy on him) was a righteous man, a scholar, proficient in the Quran; he lost his sight at the end of his life, so he occupied himself with teaching the children the Book of God, and many people memorized the Quran at his hands. He used to teach the poor purely for the sake of God, taking from them no dirham nor anything else, rather perhaps he would assist them from his own means. He was very [shakoot?] rarely speaking except occasionally, and his litany in most of his times was the prayer of Sayyidi Abd al-Salam ibn Mashish. And he used to give me glad tidings [in my youth that I would become a scholar].'
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a biographical note introducing the author of the original text, whose name is given as Ahmad ibn Muhammad (Imam al-Dardir). It then quotes from the Shaykh's commentary on his own work 'Aqrab al-Masalik' a description of his father. The description portrays the father as a righteous and learned man who was an expert in the Quran. He lost his eyesight later in life, after which he dedicated himself to teaching children the Quran, and many people memorized the Quran under his instruction. He taught the poor without any compensation, never taking even a dirham, and would sometimes support them from his own resources. He is described as being 'kathir al-shukoot' (very silent), rarely speaking. His regular devotional practice (wird) consisted largely of the prayer of Sayyidi Abd al-Salam ibn Mashish. The passage ends with the father giving the young Imam al-Dardir glad tidings that he would become a scholar.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Biographies in kalam texts are just formal names without importance.
Correction: This biography shows the deep piety and sacrifice of the father, which shaped the imam.

Checkpoint

B) He taught children the Quran for free.
C) He became a merchant.
D) He traveled for knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The father of Imam al-Dardir was a righteous blind teacher who taught the Quran for free and prayed the wird of Abd al-Salam ibn Mashish.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The father lost his sight but dedicated his life to teaching children the Quran. What have you done with the abilities you still have? Are you using them for the sake of Allah, or are you making excuses?
?
The father taught the poor without taking any payment, and even helped them from his own means. Think about your own charity and teaching—do you seek any return, even recognition? Can you truly say you give purely for Allah?
?
The father's litany was the prayer of Abd al-Salam ibn Mashish, and he was silent most of the time. What fills your time? Are you preoccupied with Allah's remembrance, or with idle talk? Commit to replacing one habit this week with that prayer or similar remembrance.

Biography of Imam al-Dardir and His Father
The narrator provides a biography and miracles of Imam al-Dardir and his father.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the life and miracles of Imam al-Dardir and his father, highlighting the honor of sainthood.

Core Idea
Sainthood shines through miracles and devotion.

This passage introduces us to the pious lineage of Imam al-Dardir. His father, a righteous scholar who lost his sight, dedicated his life to teaching the Quran for free. The narrative then recounts the remarkable miracles associated with both father and son, affirming the Islamic concept of karamat (saintly miracles). Through these stories, we glimpse the spiritual atmosphere of that era.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the biographical details and the sequence of events.
The passage begins with naming the shaykh and his father.
2
Identify key biographical facts
Extract the birth and death dates of al-Dardir, the occupation of his father, and the father's martyrdom by plague.
3
Analyze the miracles
Note the three miracles: candles from salawat, food multiplication through Surat Quraysh, and opening locked doors with the same sura. Reflect on their purpose.
These are signs of sainthood, not prophethood.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أحمد) : هو اسم الشيخ. وقوله : (ابن محمّد) هو اسم أبيه قال الشَّيخ في شرح كتابه «أقرب المسالك لمذهب الإمام مالك»: وكان الوالد الله تعالى رجلاً صالحاً عالماً متقناً للقرآن، فقد بصره في آخر عمره، فاشتغل بتعليم الأطفال كتاب الله تعالى فحفظ القرآن على يديه خلق كثير . وكان يعلّم الفقراء حسبةً لله تعالى لا يأخذ منهم صرفةً ولا غيرها، بل ربَّما واساهم من عنده وكان كثير الشكوت لا يتكلم إلا نادراً، وورده في الله غالب أوقاته صلاة سيدي عبد السَّلام بن مشيش ، وكان يبشرني في صغري بأن أكون عالماً . مات له شهيداً بالطاعون سنة ثمانية وثلاثين بعد الألف ومئة، وعمري نحو العشر سنين، وشوهدت له كرامات) انتهى (1) وحينئذٍ فيؤخذ منه أنَّ الشَّيخ ولد سنة ثمان وعشرين بعد المئة والألف، وكانت وفاته ليلة الجمعة لثمان خلون من ربيع الأول، سنة مئتين وواحد الألف، فَسِتُّه ثلاث وسبعون ودفن بمشهده المشهور بالكعكيين، وكراماته في الحياة وبعد الممات أظهر من الشَّمس في رابعة النَّهار، وأقول بعد كما قال بعض العارفين (2) [ من مجزوء الرجز] ســـادة مـــن عـــزهــــم أقــدامـهـم فـوق الــجـــاه إن لم أكن مــنــهـ فلي في حــبــهــم عــز وجــاه وأخبرنا الأستاذ الشارح عن والده المذكور: أنَّ زوجته كانت تدخل عليه فتجد عنده شموعاً موقودةً في أوقات الظلام، فتسأله عن فيقول : (إِنَّها أنوار الصَّلاة على النَّبيِّ ) . ذلك، وأخبرنا أيضاً أنَّهم كانوا في ضيق عيش، فتوضع الصَّحفة فيها الطعام القليل بين يديه فيقرأ عليها سورة (قريش) فيبارك فيها، ويأكل منها النَّاس الكثيرون، قال الشَّيخ : فصرت أقرأ تلك السُّورة على الأبواب المغلوقة فتفتح بغير مفتاح، فشاع عنّي وأنا صغير أنّي أفتح الأبواب بغير مفتاح (1) أقرب المسالك مع حاشية الصاوي (٧/١). (٢) البيتان في البرهان المؤيد (ص٢٠٧) من غير نسبة .
His saying: (Ahmad): he is the name of the Shaykh. And his saying: (ibn Muhammad) is the name of his father. The Shaykh said in the commentary on his book 'Aqrab al-Masalik li-Madhhab al-Imam Malik': The parent—may Allah Most High have mercy on him—was a righteous man, a scholar, proficient in the Quran; he lost his eyesight in his later life, so he occupied himself with teaching children the Book of Allah Most High, and many people memorized the Quran at his hands. He used to teach the poor for the sake of Allah Most High, not taking from them any payment or anything else; rather, he would sometimes help them from his own wealth. He was very silent, rarely speaking, and his wird in Allah most of his time was the prayer of Sayyidi Abd al-Salam ibn Mashish. And he used to give me glad tidings in my childhood that I would become a scholar. He died as a martyr by plague in the year 1138 AH, and I was about ten years old, and miracles were seen from him.) End. (1) Hence it is inferred that the Shaykh was born in the year 1128 AH, and his death was on the night of Friday, 8 Rabi' al-Awwal, 1201 AH, so his age was seventy-three. He was buried at his well-known shrine in al-Ka'kiyyin, and his miracles in life and after death are more manifest than the sun at midday. And I say afterward, as some arifs said: (2) [From the mujza' rajaz] Masters whose steps are above the heavens—if I am not of them, yet in loving them I have honor and status. And our teacher the commentator informed us about the aforementioned father: that his wife would come to him and find candles lit near him at times of darkness, and she would ask him about that, and he would say: 'They are lights from prayer upon the Prophet.' And he also informed us that they were in straitened circumstances, so a platter with a little food would be placed before him, and he would recite Surat Quraysh over it, and it would be blessed, and many people would eat from it. The Shaykh said: Then I began to recite that sura over locked doors, and they would open without a key, so it became famous about me while I was young that I open doors without a key. (1) Aqrab al-Masalik with Hashiyat al-Sawi (7/1). (2) The two lines are in al-Burhan al-Mu'ayyad (p. 207) without attribution.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage provides a biography of Imam al-Dardir (the author of the original text) and his father. It is a narrative that includes several elements: identification of the Shaykh's name (Ahmad ibn Muhammad), a description of his father's piety and learning (he was blind, taught Quran to children for free, was silent, and performed the prayer of Abd al-Salam ibn Mashish as his regular devotion), his father's martyrdom in the plague of 1138 AH, al-Dardir's own birth (1128 AH) and death (1201 AH at age 73), and the manifestation of his miracles. Two specific miracles are recounted: one about his father having candles lit miraculously, explaining them as lights from salawat upon the Prophet; and another about food multiplying through recitation of Surat Quraysh, and al-Dardir himself using that sura to open locked doors. The passage ends with poetic lines praising the awliya. The tone is reverent and hagiographic.

Key Terms

أحمد بن محمد
أقرب المسالك
الطاعون
كرامات
صلاة سيدي عبد السلام بن مشيش
سورة قريش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think these miracles prove prophethood.
Correction: In Islam, miracles (karamat) are signs of sainthood (wilaya), not prophethood. Prophets have mu'jizat, while saints have karamat. The passage emphasizes the spiritual status of al-Dardir and his father as saints.

Checkpoint
What was the occupation of Imam al-Dardir's father after he lost his eyesight?

B) He taught children the Quran without taking payment

The biography of Imam al-Dardir and his father illustrates the profound spiritual legacy and miraculous signs that accompany sainthood in Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The father taught the poor without any payment and even supported them from his own wealth. What is one thing you currently do—or could start doing—for the sake of Allah without expecting any worldly return, even if it costs you something? Will you commit to it this week?
?
When the father's wife saw candles lit supernaturally, he explained they were lights from sending salawat. Do you truly believe that your own recitation of salawat has a visible, tangible effect in your life? If not, what is blocking that conviction?
?
Imam al-Dardir recited Surat Quraysh over locked doors and they opened. Think of a locked door in your life—a difficulty, a sin, a broken relationship. Are you willing to recite that sura (or any Quranic verse) with full certainty that Allah can open it for you? What would that require you to let go of?

Birth, Death, and Burial of the Sheikh
Biographical dates and burial place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the biographical details of the Sheikh's birth, death, and burial place as recorded in the text.

Core Idea
A saint's life is marked by both earthly dates and divine favor.

This lesson presents the factual biography of the Sheikh drawn from the source. It establishes his birth year, death year, age at death, and burial location, providing a framework for understanding his later miraculous acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text aloud, then follow with the literal English translation. Notice the precision of the dates.
The numbers may have OCR errors; focus on the structure.
2
Identify the key biographical facts
From the passage, extract the year of death, the deduced year of birth, his age at death, and his burial place.
The passage says he died at age 73 and was buried at al-Ka'kikiyin.

Reflect on the mention of karamat
Even in the biography, the text notes 'karaamat were seen of him'. Consider why this detail is included right after the death date.
This prepares the reader for the miracle stories to come.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مات له شهيداً بالطاعون سنة ثمانية وثلاثين بعد الألف ومئة، وعمري نحو العشر سنين، وشوهدت له كرامات) انتهى (1) وحينئذٍ فيؤخذ منه أنَّ الشَّيخ ولد سنة ثمان وعشرين بعد المئة والألف، وكانت وفاته ليلة الجمعة لثمان خلون من ربيع الأول، سنة مئتين وواحد الألف، فَسِتُّه ثلاث وسبعون ودفن بمشهده المشهور بالكعكيين، وكراماته في الحياة وبعد الممات أظهر من الشَّمس في رابعة النَّهار، وأقول بعد كما قال بعض العارفين لــــي. (۲) [ من مجزوء الرجز] ســـادة مـــن عـــزهــــم أقــدامـهـم فـوق الــجـــاه إن لم أكن مــنــهـ فلي في حــبــهــم عــز وجــاه وأخبرنا الأستاذ الشارح عن والده المذكور: أنَّ زوجته كانت تدخل عليه فتجد عنده شموعاً موقودةً في أوقات الظلام، فتسأله عن فيقول : (إِنَّها أنوار الصَّلاة على النَّبيِّ ) . ذلك، وأخبرنا أيضاً أنَّهم كانوا في ضيق عيش، فتوضع الصَّحفة فيها الطعام القليل بين يديه فيقرأ عليها سورة (قريش) فيبارك فيها، ويأكل منها النَّاس الكثيرون، قال الشَّيخ : فصرت أقرأ تلك السُّورة على الأبواب المغلوقة فتفتح بغير مفتاح، فشاع عنّي وأنا صغير أنّي أفتح الأبواب بغير مفتاح
He died as a martyr by the plague in the year 838 [؟], and my age was about ten years, and karamat were seen of him. End. From this it is taken that the Sheikh was born in the year 828 [؟], and his death was on Friday night, eight days remaining of Rabi' al-Awwal, in the year 1201 [؟], so his age was seventy-three, and he was buried in his famous shrine at al-Ka'kikiyin, and his karamat in life and after death are more apparent than the sun at midday. And I say after as some of the gnostics said to me: [verse] 'Masters whose dignity their feet are above the rank, if I am not of them, then in their love I have honor and rank.' And our teacher the commentator informed us from his mentioned father: that his wife used to enter upon him and find candles lit at times of darkness, and she asked him about that, and he said: 'They are lights of the prayer upon the Prophet.' That. And he also informed us that they were in straitened circumstances, so a plate with little food was placed before him and he recited Surat Quraysh over it, and it was blessed, and many people ate from it. The Sheikh said: So I began to recite that Surah on locked doors and they would open without a key, and it became famous about me while I was young that I open doors without a key.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of a sheikh, likely al-Dardir or the author of the matn, mixing official biographical details with miraculous reports (karamat). The first part gives his date of death (plague, year unclear), his age, and his burial place. The author then inserts two lines of rajaz poetry praising saints. The second part reports two miracles transmitted from the commentator's father: the sheikh's wife saw candles that were explained as lights from prayer on the Prophet, and the sheikh recited Surat Quraysh over a small amount of food causing it to feed many. Finally, the sheikh himself tells that he used the same sura to open locked doors without a key. These stories serve to affirm the saintly status of the sheikh and the power of prayer and Qur'anic recitation.

Two karamat stories and a general statement on karamat.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
The Sheikh's karamat prove that divine power works through saintly recitation.

This lesson presents two concrete miracles reported from the Sheikh's life and family. These stories illustrate the baraka (blessing) associated with prayer upon the Prophet and recitation of Surat Quraysh.
